<div id="review_2772299257" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2772299257" />
    <a title="Schizanthus Nerd" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/52870273-schizanthus-nerd"><img alt="Schizanthus Nerd" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1588332751p2/52870273.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2772299257?book_show_action=true">Jul 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Schizanthus Nerd" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Schizanthus Nerd" href="/user/show/52870273-schizanthus-nerd">Schizanthus Nerd</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52870273-schizanthus-nerd?shelf=ugly-cry">ugly-cry</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52870273-schizanthus-nerd?shelf=kindle-food">kindle-food</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/52870273-schizanthus-nerd?shelf=content-warnings">content-warnings</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2772299257" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9598602859458721988">Do you know how difficult it is to whisper an ugly cry? I do. There I was at 3:30am, relaxed and enjoying the insight and surprising humour of this book, caught up in a ‘just one more chapter’ loop. Then, out of nowhere, I was ugly crying as quietly as possible so I didn’t wake up the sensible people in my home, those who actually sleep when it’s considered an acceptable time to do so. Okay, so it wasn’t exactly ‘out of nowhere’; I knew it was coming at some stage with that particular patient bu</span>
  <span id="freeText9598602859458721988" style="display:none">Do you know how difficult it is to whisper an ugly cry? I do. There I was at 3:30am, relaxed and enjoying the insight and surprising humour of this book, caught up in a ‘just one more chapter’ loop. Then, out of nowhere, I was ugly crying as quietly as possible so I didn’t wake up the sensible people in my home, those who actually sleep when it’s considered an acceptable time to do so. Okay, so it wasn’t exactly ‘out of nowhere’; I knew it was coming at some stage with that particular patient but I wasn’t expecting it right then. <br /><br />That wasn’t the only time I cried during this book (there may have been another four tissue grabs and some very dignified sniffling involved) and it wasn’t the only time my tears caught me off guard (who knew I’d cry about the patient I initially loved to sneer at!) but it did remind me of some of the reasons why I never formally used my psychology degree.<br /><br /><i>Reason #1</i>: Although I don’t cry a lot about my own stuff, I am a champion crier when it comes to pretty much anything else. Movies. TV shows. Songs. Books. When you cry about your stuff. When I think about your stuff and consider how brave, resilient, [insert any number of adjectives here] you were, are or are going to be. Who wants to come to therapy and feel like they need to console their therapist about their reaction to their patient’s problems?!<br /><br /><i>Reason #2</i>: There would be certain types of people and life experiences where I just know I couldn’t remain impartial. ‘Oh, so you’re a child molester? Allow me to introduce you to another patient of mine, the one that keeps getting imprisoned for assault. I’m sure you’ll get along just fine.’<br /><br /><i>Reason #3</i>: The goodbyes. See <i>Reason #1</i>.<br /><br />Full disclosure: I started reading this book while my own therapist was on leave. Besides confirming my decision to not actually be a therapist (you’re so welcome, all of the people whose lives would have crossed my path in this way. I hope you found a Wendell instead!) I also got a glimpse of what it’s like behind the scenes for therapists, something I’ve always been interested in, something that’s difficult to obtain because of that pesky ‘confidentiality’ thing.<br /><br />I’m not ashamed to say that I have my very own Wendell, who is awesome by the way. None of us get out of life unscathed and I think pretty much everyone could benefit from therapy at some point in their lives. One of the perks this book offers is a therapeutic ‘try before you buy’; if you’ve been considering therapy but are hesitant to schedule that initial appointment, then reading this book will give you some idea of what to expect - from the therapist, from the experience, and how it looks when it’s done right. <blockquote> Sitting-with-you-in-your-pain is one of the rare experiences that people get in the protected space of a therapy room, but it’s very hard to give or get outside of it </blockquote> I enjoyed Lori’s down to earth approach, her compassion and ability to bring truth to a situation, while still making me smile along the way. She humanises our experience of pain and even when she’s talking about her own therapy, her insight and openness had me smiling in recognition much more frequently than the narrative made me cry. Of her own therapy: <blockquote> Yes, I’m seeking objectivity, but only because I’m convinced that objectivity will rule in my favor. </blockquote> Of her therapist: <blockquote> He looks at me meaningfully, like he just said something incredibly important and profound, but I kind of want to punch him. </blockquote> A quote I <b>love</b>: <blockquote> <i>defenses</i> serve a useful purpose. They shield people from injury … until they no longer need them.<br />It’s in this ellipsis that therapists work. </blockquote> And another: <blockquote> People often mistake numbness for nothingness, but numbness isn’t the absence of feelings; it’s a response to being overwhelmed by too many feelings. </blockquote> Oh, and I have to share this one too: <blockquote> When the present falls apart, so does the future we had associated with it. And having the future taken away is the mother of all plot twists. But if we spend the present trying to fix the past or control the future, we remain stuck in place, in perpetual regret. </blockquote> I highlighted so many passages in this book that each time I started another binge read it felt like I was experiencing my very own mini therapy session. I saw myself in Lori and in her patients, even the initial ‘love to sneer at’ one, probably because I saw something of myself in them as well. I saw my own therapist in Wendell and felt probably too much pride in having found myself such an amazing ‘Wendell’ to help me navigate my presenting problem as well as the real issues behind the facade.<br /><br />From the presenting problem to the “doorknob disclosures”, “what-aboutery” and self-sabotage, all the way to the “termination” (seriously, can therapists collectively find a less aggressive way to label someone’s graduation from therapy?), I ‘just one more chaptered’ my way through this book. <br /><br />Although at times I felt voyeuristic, have some outstanding questions about Lori’s patients I’m not entitled to know but still want to (Would you please tell me John’s real name or at least the name of the TV show you kept referencing so I can binge watch it?) and had at least one ugly cry headache as a result of reading this book, I wouldn’t hesitate to recommend it to pretty much anyone. <br /><br />Much like the way Lori talks about who therapy can’t help, I think the only people who wouldn’t benefit in some way by reading this book are those “who aren’t curious about themselves.” I’ll leave you with what’s currently my favourite quote: <blockquote> There’s no hierarchy of pain. Suffering shouldn’t be ranked, because pain is not a contest. </blockquote> <i>Content warnings</i> include mention of suicidal ideation, addiction, mental health, grief, chronic illness and a whole pile of other reasons people seek therapy in the first place.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9598602859458721988" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2782609081" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer271849266038586506"><i>Maybe You Should Talk to Someone</i> is by therapist Lori Gottlieb who experienced an unexpected breakup in her 40s and decided to go to therapy herself as part of her healing process. In this book, she shares her thoughts and experiences as well as the journeys of a handful of her own patients, each working through different challenges. <br/><br/>Lori dipped into a few careers before finding her calling in therapy. I appreciated that she was selective in choosing her own therapist as well. While there are ma</span>
  <span id="freeText271849266038586506" style="display:none"><i>Maybe You Should Talk to Someone</i> is by therapist Lori Gottlieb who experienced an unexpected breakup in her 40s and decided to go to therapy herself as part of her healing process. In this book, she shares her thoughts and experiences as well as the journeys of a handful of her own patients, each working through different challenges. <br /><br />Lori dipped into a few careers before finding her calling in therapy. I appreciated that she was selective in choosing her own therapist as well. While there are many great medical professionals today, it baffles me how often patients don’t do their own due diligence — It’s your health, be critical! <br /><br />We’re all looking for answers, though the answers we desire aren’t always the same. Therapy can help guide people as a tool to ask themselves the right questions and be truthful about their answers. <br /><br /><i>Maybe You Should Talk To Someone</i> was so interesting, both Lori’s personal journey and the winding paths of her various patients. We all struggle sometimes — Knowing when to ask for help and seek guidance is important. I appreciated the significant “human” element throughout this book and while certain patients/experiences may resonate more than others, there’s a takeaway here for everyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="271849266038586506" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3258733750804468117">Not my normal genre - I don't read much non-fiction and no self-help, but I loved this.<br/><br/>I adore books that make me laugh and cry, teach me things and make me think about stuff I've never thought of before.   <span id="freeText3258733750804468117" style="display:none">Not my normal genre - I don't read much non-fiction and no self-help, but I loved this.<br /><br />I adore books that make me laugh and cry, teach me things and make me think about stuff I've never thought of before. Maybe You Should Talk to Someone ticked all of the above boxes.<br /><br />Lori Gottlieb shows us that to make mistakes is human, and that we should have more compassion for others AND ourselves.<br /><br />I loved that we got to know the author, her therapist and her selected patients on a deeply personal level, and that I came to care about and learn from each of them.<br /><br />I listened to the audio and can highly recommend it, the narrator felt like she was talking to me and not reading something.<br /><br />Please, please tell me that the author will be publishing more of these focusing on other patients. Also please let me know if you can recommend something similar.</span>

<div id="review_3341812955" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3341812955" />
    <a title="jenny✨" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5160409-jenny"><img alt="jenny✨" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1534561275p2/5160409.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3341812955?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="jenny✨" class="user" itemprop="url" name="jenny✨" href="/user/show/5160409-jenny">jenny✨</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=2019">2019</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=psychology">psychology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=mental-health-and-illness">mental-health-and-illness</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=non-fic">non-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=growth-love-n-relationships">growth-love-n-relationships</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5160409-jenny?shelf=auto-biography-memoir">auto-biography-memoir</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3341812955" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2679688606430823724"><i>
  <blockquote>Maybe we all need to doubt, rail against, and question before we can really let go.</blockquote>
</i><br/>⭐️⭐️⭐️✨ <b>3.5 stars!</b> <br/><br/>I have to admit, sometimes this book REALLY screamed “Hello world, I counsel rich white Los Angeleans!” and I couldn’t <i>quite</i> into the flow of it. The author’s personal anecdotes alternate somewhat jarringly with those of four patients, and I found myself setting this book aside several times over the course of reading it. <br/><br/><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2861974489' rel='nofollow noopener'>Another reviewer</a> mentioned that the author comes off as “self-indulgen</span>
  <span id="freeText2679688606430823724" style="display:none"><i>
  <blockquote>Maybe we all need to doubt, rail against, and question before we can really let go.</blockquote>
</i><br />⭐️⭐️⭐️✨ <b>3.5 stars!</b> <br /><br />I have to admit, sometimes this book REALLY screamed “Hello world, I counsel rich white Los Angeleans!” and I couldn’t <i>quite</i> into the flow of it. The author’s personal anecdotes alternate somewhat jarringly with those of four patients, and I found myself setting this book aside several times over the course of reading it. <br /><br /><a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2861974489" rel="nofollow noopener">Another reviewer</a> mentioned that the author comes off as “self-indulgent, self-obsessed and, weirdly--NOT FUNNY,” and… yeah. To a certain extent, that captures my impression of this book. Some moments of poignancy didn’t hit me as hard as I’d wanted/expected (not a comment on the content of people’s lives, just the <b>canned, pallid quality to Gottlieb's writing</b>), while some of the amusing parts weren’t particularly <i>amusing</i> (to me, at least. I’m assuming someone who shops at Anthropologie and Whole Foods on the reg will probably connect more to certain parts?). With its absurd length (415 pages for a memoir-type book!?), <b><i>self-indulgent</i> was precisely the term that kept popping into my head as I read.</b><br /><br />I also kept myself on tenterhooks throughout the book, anticipating a radical shift in thinking or at least some sort of emotional breakthrough—but neither really happened. And maybe these are unfairly high expectations. I think this book may merit a reread in future, to allow its insights to really process/percolate/sink in for me. <br /><br />I have the feeling that, given the chance, this book could be a sleeper hit for me.<br /><br />◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️◻️<br /><br /><b>Yet embedded within this book are numerous nuggets of invaluable (and immediate) wisdom. </b> One of my favourite chapters was “What Not to Say to a Dying Person” (don’t: say “Everything happens for a reason”; do: be genuine—even if it means admitting you don’t know how to respond). I adored the book’s <b>ambivalence, its emphasis on the duality of our experiences:</b> to erase pain is to also mute joy; and closure is a myth—rather than coming to an insightful, conclusive end, we must find meaning in the <i>middles</i> of our stories. <br /><br />As I read, I was struck by a particular passage: <br /><i>
  <blockquote>The therapist Terry Real described our well-worn behaviors as “our internalized family of origin. It’s our repertoire of relational themes.” People don’t have to tell you their stories with words because they always act them out for you. Often they project negative expectations onto the therapist, but if the therapist doesn’t meet those negative expectations, this “corrective emotional experience” with a reliable and benevolent person changes the patients; the world, they learn, turns out not to be their family of origin.</blockquote>
</i><br />If there’s something you should know about me, it’s that I avoid confrontation like I’m playing a cosmic game of confrontation dodgeball. Even when I’m burning to say something—knowing full well that such honesty, though initially hurtful, would absolutely benefit both me and my friend in the long run—I don’t. <br /><br /><b>But this passage helped me reframe my thoughts about confrontation and voicing unpopular (though necessary) opinions.</b> Maybe my friend who’s head-over-heels enamoured with the toxic dude needs to hear me say that he’s not a good guy because she’s never had anyone challenge her expectations otherwise. Maybe my friend who’s constantly pessimistic needs to hear that she, too, deserves joy. If I don’t meet their “negative expectations”—approving of the horrible boyfriend, encouraging the cynical ranting—maybe I will be able to help my friends the way I want to. Even if it’s hard. Even if I'm going to have to be braver than I've ever been before.<br /><br /><b>
  <u>Bottom line:</u>
</b> Though I found the prose a little bland and self-indulgent at times, this book is still absolutely worth a read for its nuanced wisdoms.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2679688606430823724" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14848185367488972257">I'm a therapist... blah blah... My boyfriend broke up with me... blah blah... My patient did this and that... blah blah. The author is spilling words on paper without putting much thought to it, so it reads like a stream of consciousness done for therapeutic reasons - and then edited here and there to make it fit for publishing and getting money of it. The book lacks structure and deeper insights, but most of all it lacks credibility. You want to believe Lori's story but she never opens up compl</span>
  <span id="freeText14848185367488972257" style="display:none">I'm a therapist... blah blah... My boyfriend broke up with me... blah blah... My patient did this and that... blah blah. The author is spilling words on paper without putting much thought to it, so it reads like a stream of consciousness done for therapeutic reasons - and then edited here and there to make it fit for publishing and getting money of it. The book lacks structure and deeper insights, but most of all it lacks credibility. You want to believe Lori's story but she never opens up completely for the reader. You want to believe her patients' stories but so many details were changed (made up even) to protect their privacy that they could as well be fictional characters. And while the author clearly enjoys writing, I wish she plucked up the courage to write a novel rather than this self-censored half journal half self-help book. Or maybe she should have written this parental book after all (wink wink).</span>

  <span id="freeText1155509425626203624" style="display:none">[4+] I doubt that I would have read this if my book club had not chosen it as our selection. The idea of listening to a therapist talk about her patients doesn't appeal to me. But I enjoyed every minute! I like Gottlieb's writing style; she has an excellent sense of pacing and draws the reader into her patients' lives and her own life without making the book feel voyeuristic. <br /><br />I was very moved by several of the stories. I feel that one of the reasons the book worked so well was the openness Gottlieb has about her own life. I had to search out how the author handled confidentiality online, because the author's note is not included in the audiobook.</span>

<div id="review_2901166370" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2901166370" />
    <a title="Antigone" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/22009196-antigone"><img alt="Antigone" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1383249021p2/22009196.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2901166370?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Antigone" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Antigone" href="/user/show/22009196-antigone">Antigone</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/22009196-antigone?shelf=memoir-biography">memoir-biography</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2901166370" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6985584863960957350">In an account that could easily be subtitled <i>Therapists Are People, Too</i>, Lori Gottlieb journeys through her experience as a Los Angeles psychotherapist practicing amidst the misery of a sudden and unexpected break with the man she had planned to marry. This necessitates her own entry into treatment and provides the reader with a unique double-field of revelation - the mechanics of analysis as encountered from both sides of that metaphorical couch.<br/><br/>Gottlieb is a fine writer. The many years she's s</span>
  <span id="freeText6985584863960957350" style="display:none">In an account that could easily be subtitled <i>Therapists Are People, Too</i>, Lori Gottlieb journeys through her experience as a Los Angeles psychotherapist practicing amidst the misery of a sudden and unexpected break with the man she had planned to marry. This necessitates her own entry into treatment and provides the reader with a unique double-field of revelation - the mechanics of analysis as encountered from both sides of that metaphorical couch.<br /><br />Gottlieb is a fine writer. The many years she's spent working as a therapist allow her to deliver insights into the process that are astute and eminently informative. Her clients come alive on the page, her empathy and self-identification easing their ordeals into our perception in a manner that humanizes the profession to a startlingly effective degree. Her own analysis is conveyed with great honesty; rich in emotion and vulnerability, all of which is captured as she struggles to discard a future she longed for but can now no longer achieve.<br /><br />This is an admirable memoir - accessible, meaningful, page-turning. It was also, for me, a little unsettling. Think of the moment Toto draws the curtain back to reveal the mortal man behind the Wizard of Emerald City and you will have my sense of it. Still, that <i>is</i> the destination of all therapeutic endeavor, I imagine. Sighting the true identity of the person one has been sharing one's thoughts with. Two humans being. And that is what Lori Gottlieb presents to us here.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6985584863960957350" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('Mt/Km65mpec6ENEXC1aqKM1wBWpAbBzghf6FkdvucboI+aNJFThsj507ZXb8o6D3Gy/ioo7hkofegMMgFq2DIA==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

Thousands wrote to say that she had given them the courage to share their own experiences of assault for the first time.\n\nNow she reclaims her identity to tell her story of trauma, transcendence, and the power of words. It was the perfect case, in many ways–there were eyewitnesses, Turner ran away, physical evidence was immediately secured. But her struggles with isolation and shame during the aftermath and the trial reveal the oppression victims face in even the best-case scenarios. Her story illuminates a culture biased to protect perpetrators, indicts a criminal justice system designed to fail the most vulnerable, and, ultimately, shines with the courage required to move through suffering and live a full and beautiful life.\n\nKnow My Name will forever transform the way we think about sexual assault, challenging our beliefs about what is acceptable and speaking truth to the tumultuous reality of healing. At eighteen, she met Tod Hostetter at a local dance, having no idea that the mercurial charmer she would impulsively wed was heir to one of the wealthiest families in America. But when he died twelve years later, Allene packed her bags for New York City. Never once did she look back.\n\nFrom the vantage point of the American upper class, Allene embodied the tumultuous Gilded Age. Over the course of four more marriages, she weathered personal tragedies during World War I and the catastrophic financial reversals of the crash of 1929. From the castles and châteaus of Europe, she witnessed the Russian Revolution and became a princess. And from the hopes of a young girl from Jamestown, New York, Allene Tew would become the epitome of both a pursuer and survivor of the American Dream.<\/span>\n  <a <span id="freeTextContainer13563017077448838954">LORI GOTTLIEB is a psychotherapist and New York Times bestselling author of MAYBE YOU SHOULD TALK TO SOMEONE, which is being adapted for TV with Eva Longoria. In addition to her clinical practice, she writes The Atlantic's weekly "Dear Therapist" advice column and contributes regularly to the New York Times. She is sought-after in media such as The Today Show, Good Morning America, CBS This Mornin</span>
  <span id="freeText13563017077448838954" style="display:none">LORI GOTTLIEB is a psychotherapist and New York Times bestselling author of MAYBE YOU SHOULD TALK TO SOMEONE, which is being adapted for TV with Eva Longoria. In addition to her clinical practice, she writes The Atlantic's weekly "Dear Therapist" advice column and contributes regularly to the New York Times. She is sought-after in media such as The Today Show, Good Morning America, CBS This Morning, CNN, and NPR's "Fresh Air." <br /><br />Learn more at LoriGottlieb.com or by following her @LoriGottlieb1 on Twitter.</span>
